href="https://www.wellsfargo.com/com/securities/">www.wellsfargo.com/com/securities/</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG/170px-NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="261" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="2736" data-file-height="4206"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 170px;height: 261px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG/170px-NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="170" data-height="261" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NewYorkSeagram_04.30.2008.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Seagram_Building" title="Seagram Building">Seagram Building</a>: Home of Wells Fargo Securities' New York offices and trading floors</div></div></div>
<p>Wells Fargo Securities (WFS) is the <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment banking</a> division of Wells Fargo &amp; Co. The size and financial performance of this group is not disclosed publicly, but analysts believe the investment banking group houses approximately 4,500 employees and generates between US$3 and US$4 billion per year in investment banking revenue. By comparison, two of Wells Fargo's largest competitors, <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America" title="Bank of America">Bank of America</a> and <a href="/wiki/J.P._Morgan_Chase" class="mw-redirect" title="J.P. Morgan Chase">J.P. Morgan Chase</a> generated approximately US$5.5 billion and US$6 billion respectively in 2011 (not including <a href="/wiki/Sales_and_trading" title="Sales and trading">sales and trading</a> revenue).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-72">[72]</a></sup> WFS headquarters are in Charlotte, North Carolina, with other US offices in New York, Minneapolis, Boston, Houston, San Francisco, and Los Angeles, with international offices in London, Hong Kong, Singapore, and Tokyo.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Cross-selling">Cross-selling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cross-selling" data-section="16" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>A key part of Wells Fargo's business strategy is <a href="/wiki/Cross-selling" title="Cross-selling">cross-selling</a>, the practice of encouraging existing customers to buy additional banking services.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Customers inquiring about their checking account balance may be pitched mortgage deals and mortgage holders may be pitched credit card offers in an attempt to increase the customer's profitability to the bank.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ-HardSell_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-HardSell-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forbes-ArtOfTheCrossSell_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-ArtOfTheCrossSell-75">[75]</a></sup> Other banks have attempted to emulate Wells Fargo's cross-selling practices (described by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> as a <a href="/wiki/Hard_sell" title="Hard sell">hard sell</a> technique);<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ-HardSell_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-HardSell-74">[74]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> magazine describes Wells Fargo as "better than anyone" at the practice.<sup id="cite_ref-Forbes-ArtOfTheCrossSell_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-ArtOfTheCrossSell-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="International_operations">International operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: International operations" data-section="17" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Wells Fargo has banking services throughout the world, with offices in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dubai" title="Dubai">Dubai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> They operate back-offices in India and the Philippines with more than 3,000 staff.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p><p>In 2010, hedge fund administrator <a href="/wiki/Citco" title="Citco">Citco</a> purchased the trust company operation of Wells Fargo in the <a href="/wiki/Cayman_Islands" title="Cayman Islands">Cayman Islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Charter">Charter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Charter" data-section="18" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Wells Fargo operates under Charter #1, the first national bank <a href="/wiki/Charter" title="Charter">charter</a> issued in the United States. This charter was issued to <a href="/wiki/First_National_Bank_(Philadelphia)" title="First National Bank (Philadelphia)">First National Bank</a> of Philadelphia on June 20, 1863, by the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_Comptroller_of_the_Currency" title="Office of the Comptroller of the Currency">Office of the Comptroller of the Currency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Traditionally, acquiring banks assume the earliest issued charter number. Thus, the first charter passed from First National Bank of Philadelphia to Wells Fargo through its 2008 acquisition of Wachovia, which had inherited it through one of its many acquisitions.
</p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(3)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span id="Lawsuits.2C_fines_and_controversies"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lawsuits,_fines_and_controversies">Lawsuits, fines and controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Lawsuits, fines and controversies" data-section="19" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-3 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-3">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Logan,_Utah_corner_of_Main_%26_Center_St.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Logan%2C_Utah_corner_of_Main_%26_Center_St.jpg/200px-Logan%2C_Utah_corner_of_Main_%26_Center_St.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="229" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="799" data-file-height="915"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 200px;height: 229px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Logan%2C_Utah_corner_of_Main_%26_Center_St.jpg/200px-Logan%2C_Utah_corner_of_Main_%26_Center_St.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="200" data-height="229" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Logan,_Utah_corner_of_Main_%26_Center_St.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Wells Fargo branch in <a href="/wiki/Logan,_Utah" title="Logan, Utah">Logan, Utah</a></div></div></div>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="1981_MAPS_Wells_Fargo_embezzlement_scandal">1981 MAPS Wells Fargo embezzlement scandal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: 1981 MAPS Wells Fargo embezzlement scandal" data-section="20" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 1981, it was discovered that a Wells Fargo assistant operations officer, Lloyd Benjamin "Ben" Lewis, had perpetrated one of the largest embezzlements in history, through its Beverly Drive branch. During 1978 - 1981, Lewis had successfully written phony debit and credit receipts to benefit <a href="/wiki/Boxing_promoter" class="mw-redirect" title="Boxing promoter">boxing promoters</a> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Rossfields_Smith&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Harold Rossfields Smith (page does not exist)">Harold J. Smith</a> (<a href="/wiki/Given_name" title="Given name">né</a> Ross Eugene Fields) and Sam "Sammie" Marshall, chairman and president, respectively, of Muhammed Ali Professional Sports, Inc. (MAPS), of which Lewis was also listed as a director; Marshall, too, was a former employee of the same Wells Fargo branch as Lewis. In excess of US$300,000 was paid to Lewis, who pled guilty to <a href="/wiki/Embezzlement" title="Embezzlement">embezzlement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conspiracy" title="Conspiracy">conspiracy</a> charges in 1981, and testified against his co-conspirators for a reduced five-year sentence.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> (Boxer <a href="/wiki/Muhammed_Ali" class="mw-redirect" title="Muhammed Ali">Muhammed Ali</a> had received a fee for the use of his name, and had no other involvement with the organization.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>)
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Higher_costs_charged_to_African-American_and_Hispanic_borrowers">Higher costs charged to African-American and Hispanic borrowers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Higher costs charged to African-American and Hispanic borrowers" data-section="21" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Illinois Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Lisa_Madigan" title="Lisa Madigan">Lisa Madigan</a> filed suit against Wells Fargo on July 31, 2009, alleging that the bank steers <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African Americans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Hispanic and Latino American">Hispanics</a> into high-cost <a href="/wiki/Subprime" class="mw-redirect" title="Subprime">subprime</a> loans. A Wells Fargo spokesman responded that "The policies, systems, and controls we have in place – including in Illinois – ensure race is not a factor..."<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> An affidavit filed in the case stated that loan officers had referred to black mortgage-seekers as "mud people," and the subprime loans as "ghetto loans." <sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> According to Beth Jacobson, a loan officer at Wells Fargo interviewed for a report in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, "We just went right after them. Wells Fargo mortgage had an emerging-markets unit that specifically targeted black churches, because it figured church leaders had a lot of influence and could convince congregants to take out subprime loans." The report goes on to present data from the city of <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, where "more than half the properties subject to foreclosure on a Wells Fargo loan from 2005 to 2008 now stand vacant. And 71 percent of those are in predominantly black neighborhoods."<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Wells Fargo agreed to pay US$125 million to subprime borrowers and US$50 million in direct down payment assistance in certain areas, for a total of US$175 million.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Failure_to_monitor_suspected_money_laundering">Failure to monitor suspected money laundering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Failure to monitor suspected money laundering" data-section="22" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In a March 2010 agreement with US federal prosecutors, Wells Fargo acknowledged that between 2004 and 2007 <a href="/wiki/Wachovia" title="Wachovia">Wachovia</a> had failed to monitor and report suspected money laundering by narcotics traffickers, including the cash used to buy four planes that shipped a total of 22 tons of cocaine into Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Overdraft_fees">Overdraft fees</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Overdraft fees" data-section="23" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In August 2010, Wells Fargo was fined by <a href="/wiki/US_District_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="US District Court">US District Court</a> judge <a href="/wiki/William_Alsup" class="mw-redirect" title="William Alsup">William Alsup</a> for overdraft practices designed to "gouge" consumers and "profiteer" at their expense, and for misleading consumers about how the bank processed transactions and assessed overdraft fees.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Settlement_and_fines_regarding_mortgage_servicing_practices">Settlement and fines regarding mortgage servicing practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Settlement and fines regarding mortgage servicing practices" data-section="24" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On February 9, 2012, it was announced that the five largest <a href="/wiki/Mortgage_servicers" class="mw-redirect" title="Mortgage servicers">mortgage servicers</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ally_Financial" title="Ally Financial">Ally Financial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America" title="Bank of America">Bank of America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Citi" class="mw-redirect" title="Citi">Citi</a>, <a href="/wiki/JPMorgan_Chase" title="JPMorgan Chase">JPMorgan Chase</a>, and Wells Fargo) agreed to a settlement with the US Federal Government and 49 states.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The settlement, known as the <a href="/wiki/National_Mortgage_Settlement" class="mw-redirect" title="National Mortgage Settlement">National Mortgage Settlement</a> (NMS), required the servicers to provide about US$26 billion in relief to distressed homeowners and in direct payments to the federal and state governments. This settlement amount makes the NMS the second largest civil settlement in US history, only trailing the <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_Master_Settlement_Agreement" title="Tobacco Master Settlement Agreement">Tobacco Master Settlement Agreement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The five banks were also required to comply with 305 new mortgage servicing standards. <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a> held out and agreed to settle with the banks separately.
</p><p>On April 5, 2012, a federal judge ordered Wells Fargo to pay US$3.1 million in punitive damages over a single loan, one of the largest fines for a bank ever for mortgaging service misconduct.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Elizabeth Magner, a federal bankruptcy judge in the Eastern District of Louisiana, cited the bank's behavior as "highly reprehensible",<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> stating that Wells Fargo has taken advantage of borrowers who rely on the bank's accurate calculations. She went on to add, "perhaps more disturbing is Wells Fargo's refusal to voluntarily correct its errors. It prefers to rely on the ignorance of borrowers or their inability to fund a challenge to its demands, rather than voluntarily relinquish gains obtained through improper accounting methods."<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="SEC_fine_due_to_inadequate_risk_disclosures">SEC fine due to inadequate risk disclosures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: SEC fine due to inadequate risk disclosures" data-section="25" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On August 14, 2012, Wells Fargo agreed to pay around US$6.5 million to settle <a href="/wiki/US_Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="US Securities and Exchange Commission">US Securities and Exchange Commission</a> (SEC) charges that in 2007 it sold risky <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a> without fully realizing their dangers.<sup id="cite_ref-Blumenthal_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blumenthal-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Lawsuit_by_FHA_over_loan_underwriting">Lawsuit by FHA over loan underwriting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Lawsuit by FHA over loan underwriting" data-section="26" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On October 9, 2012, the US Federal Government sued the bank under the <a href="/wiki/False_Claims_Act" title="False Claims Act">False Claims Act</a> at the federal court in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>. The suit alleges that Wells Fargo defrauded the US <a href="/wiki/Federal_Housing_Administration" title="Federal Housing Administration">Federal Housing Administration</a> (FHA) over the past ten years, underwriting over 100,000 FHA backed loans when over half did not qualify for the program. This suit is the third allegation levied against Wells Fargo in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p><p>In October 2012, Wells Fargo was sued by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney" title="United States Attorney">United States Attorney</a> <a href="/wiki/Preet_Bharara" title="Preet Bharara">Preet Bharara</a> over questionable mortgage deals.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Lawsuit_due_to_premium_inflation_on_forced_place_insurance">Lawsuit due to premium inflation on forced place insurance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Lawsuit due to premium inflation on forced place insurance" data-section="27" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In April 2013, Wells Fargo settled a suit with 24,000 Florida homeowners alongside insurer <a href="/wiki/QBE_Insurance" title="QBE Insurance">QBE</a>, in which Wells Fargo was accused of inflating premiums on forced-place insurance.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Lawsuit_regarding_excessive_overdraft_fees">Lawsuit regarding excessive overdraft fees</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Lawsuit regarding excessive overdraft fees" data-section="28" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In May 2013, Wells Fargo paid US$203 million to settle class-action litigation accusing the bank of imposing excessive <a href="/wiki/Overdraft" title="Overdraft">overdraft</a> fees on checking-account customers. Also in May, the New York attorney-general, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Schneiderman" title="Eric Schneiderman">Eric Schneiderman</a>, announced a lawsuit against Wells Fargo over alleged violations of the national mortgage settlement, a US$25 billion deal struck between 49 state attorneys and the five-largest mortgage servicers in the US. Schneidermann claimed Wells Fargo had violated rules over giving fair and timely serving.<sup id="cite_ref-euromoney1_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euromoney1-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="2015_Violation_of_New_York_credit_card_laws">2015 Violation of New York credit card laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: 2015 Violation of New York credit card laws" data-section="29" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In February 2015, Wells Fargo agreed to pay US$4 million for violations where an affiliate took interest in the homes of borrowers in exchange for opening credit card accounts for the homeowners. This is illegal according to New York credit card laws. There was a US$2 million penalty with the other US$2 million going towards restitution to customers.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Executive_compensation">Executive compensation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Executive compensation" data-section="30" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>With CEO John Stumpf being paid 473 times more than the median employee, Wells Fargo ranks number 33 among the S&amp;P 500 companies for CEO—employee pay inequality. In October 2014, a Wells Fargo employee earning US$15 per hour emailed the CEO—copying 200,000 other employees—asking that all employees be given a US$10,000 per year raise taken from a portion of annual corporate profits to address wage stagnation and income inequality. After being contacted by the media, Wells Fargo responded that all employees receive "market competitive" pay and benefits significantly above US federal minimums.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Tax_avoidance_and_lobbying">Tax avoidance and lobbying</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Tax avoidance and lobbying" data-section="31" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In December 2011, the non-partisan organization <a href="/wiki/Public_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Public Campaign">Public Campaign</a> criticized Wells Fargo for spending US$11 million on <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobbying</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tax_avoidance" title="Tax avoidance">not paying any taxes</a> during 2008–2010, instead getting US$681 million in tax rebates, despite making a profit of US$49 billion, laying off 6,385 workers since 2008, and increasing executive pay by 180% to US$49.8 million in 2010 for its top five executives.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> As of 2014 however, at an effective tax rate of 31.2% of its income, Wells Fargo is the fourth-largest payer of corporation tax in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Prison_industry_investment">Prison industry investment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Prison industry investment" data-section="32" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prison%E2%80%93industrial_complex" title="Prison–industrial complex">Prison–industrial complex</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/GEO_Group" title="GEO Group">GEO Group</a>, Inc., a multi-national provider of for-profit <a href="/wiki/Private_prison" title="Private prison">private prisons</a>, received investments made by Wells Fargo mutual funds on behalf of clients, not investments made by Wells Fargo and Company, according to company statements.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> By March 2012, its stake had grown to more than 4.4 million shares worth US$86.7 million.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> As of November, 2012, the latest SEC filings reveal that Wells Fargo has divested 33% of its dispositive holdings of GEO's stock, which reduces Wells Fargo's holdings to 4.98% of Geo Group's common stock. By reducing its holdings to less than 5%, Wells Fargo will no longer be required to disclose some financial dealings with GEO.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p><p>While a coalition of organizations, National People's Action Campaign, have seen some success in pressuring Wells Fargo to divest from private prison companies like GEO Group, the company continues to make such investments.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="SEC_settlement_for_insider_trading_case">SEC settlement for insider trading case</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: SEC settlement for insider trading case" data-section="33" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2015, an analyst at Wells Fargo settled an insider trading case with the US Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC). The former employee was charged with insider trading alongside an ex-Wells Fargo trader.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sadis_%26_Goldberg" title="Sadis &amp; Goldberg">Sadis &amp; Goldberg</a> obtained a settlement that permitted the client to continue in securities industry, while neither admitting nor denying one charge of negligence-based § 17(a)(3) claim, and paying a US$75,000 civil penalty<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Wells_Fargo_fake_accounts_scandal">Wells Fargo fake accounts scandal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Wells Fargo fake accounts scandal" data-section="34" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wells_Fargo_account_fraud_scandal" title="Wells Fargo account fraud scandal">Wells Fargo account fraud scandal</a></div>
<p>In September 2016, Wells Fargo was issued a combined total of US$185 million in fines for opening over 1.5 million checking and savings accounts and 500,000 credit cards on behalf of their customers, without their consent. The US <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Financial_Protection_Bureau" title="Consumer Financial Protection Bureau">Consumer Financial Protection Bureau</a> issued US$100 million in fines, the largest in the agency's five-year history, along with US$50 million in fines from the City and County of Los Angeles, and US$35 million in fines from the Office of Comptroller of the Currency.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The scandal was caused by an incentive-compensation program for employees to create new accounts. It led to the firing of nearly 5,300 employees and US$5 million being set aside for customer refunds on fees for accounts the customers never wanted.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carrie_Tolstedt" title="Carrie Tolstedt">Carrie Tolstedt</a>, who headed the department, retired in July 2016 and received US$124.6 million in stock, options, and restricted Wells Fargo shares as a retirement package.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> On October 12, 2016, <a href="/wiki/John_Stumpf" title="John Stumpf">John Stumpf</a>, the then Chairman and CEO, announced that he would be retiring amidst the controversies involving his company. It was announced by Wells Fargo that President and Chief Operating Officer <a href="/wiki/Timothy_J._Sloan" title="Timothy J. Sloan">Timothy J. Sloan</a> would succeed, effective immediately.  Following the scandal, applications for credit cards and checking accounts at the bank plummeted.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>  In response to the event, the <a href="/wiki/Better_Business_Bureau" title="Better Business Bureau">Better Business Bureau</a> dropped accreditation of the bank,<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/S%26P_Global" title="S&amp;P Global">S&amp;P Global Ratings</a> lowered its outlook for Wells Fargo from stable to negative,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> and several states and cities across the US ended business relations with the company.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>  An investigation by the Wells Fargo board of directors, the report of which was released in April 2017, primarily blamed Stumpf, whom it said had not responded to evidence of wrongdoing in the consumer services division, and Tolstedt, who was said to have knowingly set impossible sales goals and refused to respond when subordinates disagreed with them.<sup id="cite_ref-clawback_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clawback-120">[120]</a></sup> The board chose to use a <a href="/wiki/Clawback" title="Clawback">clawback</a> clause in the retirement contracts of Stumpf and Tolstedt to recover US$75 million worth of cash and stock from the former executives.<sup id="cite_ref-clawback_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clawback-120">[120]</a></sup></p><p>Wells Fargo reached a settlement with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">United States Department of Justice</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Securities and Exchange Commission">Securities and Exchange Commission</a> in February 2020.  The settlement, which included a <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$3 billion</span> fine for the bank's violations of criminal and civil law, does not prevent individual employees from being targets of future litigation.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Racketeering_lawsuit_for_mortgage_appraisal_overcharges">Racketeering lawsuit for mortgage appraisal overcharges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Racketeering lawsuit for mortgage appraisal overcharges" data-section="35" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In November 2016, Wells Fargo agreed to pay US$50 million to settle a racketeering lawsuit in which the bank was accused of overcharging hundreds of thousands of homeowners for appraisals ordered after they defaulted on their mortgage loans.  While banks are allowed to charge homeowners for such appraisals, Wells Fargo frequently charged homeowners US$95 to US$125 on appraisals for which the bank had been charged US$50 or less.  The plaintiffs had sought triple damages under the U S <a href="/wiki/Racketeer_Influenced_and_Corrupt_Organizations_Act" title="Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act">Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act</a> on grounds that sending invoices and statements with fraudulently concealed fees constituted mail and wire fraud sufficient to allege racketeering.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Dakota_Access_Pipeline_investment">Dakota Access Pipeline investment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Dakota Access Pipeline investment" data-section="36" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Wells Fargo is a lender on the <a href="/wiki/Dakota_Access_Pipeline" title="Dakota Access Pipeline">Dakota Access Pipeline</a>, a 1,172-mile-long (1,886 km) underground oil <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipeline transport</a> system in <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>. The pipeline has been controversial regarding its potential impact on the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p><p>In February 2017, <a href="/wiki/Seattle,_Washington" class="mw-redirect" title="Seattle, Washington">Seattle, Washington</a>'s city council unanimously voted to not renew its contract with Wells Fargo "in a move that cites the bank's role as a lender to the Dakota Access Pipeline project as well as its "creation of millions of bogus accounts." and saying the bidding process for its next banking partner will involve "social responsibility." The City Council of <a href="/wiki/Davis,_California" title="Davis, California">Davis, California</a>, took a similar action voting unanimously to find a new bank to handle its accounts by the end of 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-NPR_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Failure_to_comply_with_document_security_requirements">Failure to comply with document security requirements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Failure to comply with document security requirements" data-section="37" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In December 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Industry_Regulatory_Authority" title="Financial Industry Regulatory Authority">Financial Industry Regulatory Authority</a> fined Wells Fargo US$5.5 million for failing to store electronic documents in a "write once, read many" format, which makes it impossible to alter or destroy records after they are written.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Connections_to_the_gun_industry_and_NRA">Connections to the gun industry and NRA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Connections to the gun industry and NRA" data-section="38" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Wells Fargo is the top banker for US gun makers and the <a href="/wiki/National_Rifle_Association" title="National Rifle Association">National Rifle Association</a> (NRA). From December 2012 through February 2018 it reportedly helped two of the biggest firearms and ammunition companies obtain US$431.1 million in loans and bonds. It also created a US$28-million line of credit for the NRA and operates the organization's primary accounts.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-126">[126]</a></sup></p><p>In a March 2018 statement Wells Fargo said, "Any solutions on how to address this epidemic will be complicated. This is why our company believes the best way to make progress on these issues is through the political and legislative process. ... We plan to engage our customers that legally manufacture firearms and other stakeholders on what we can do together to promote better gun safety for our communities." <sup id="cite_ref-auto_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-126">[126]</a></sup> Wells Fargo's CEO subsequently said that the bank would provide its gun clients with feedback from employees and  investors.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Discrimination_against_female_workers">Discrimination against female workers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Discrimination against female workers" data-section="39" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Glass_ceiling" title="Glass ceiling">Glass ceiling</a></div>
<p>In June 2018, about a dozen female Wells Fargo executives from the wealth management division met in Scottsdale, Arizona to discuss the minimal presence of women occupying senior roles within the company. The meeting, dubbed "the meeting of 12", represented the majority of the regional managing directors, of which 12 out of 45 are women.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Wells Fargo had previously been investigating reports of gender bias in the division in the months leading up to the meeting.<sup id="cite_ref-Rooney_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rooney-129">[129]</a></sup> The women reported that they had been turned down for top jobs despite their qualifications, and instead the roles were occupied by men.<sup id="cite_ref-Rooney_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rooney-129">[129]</a></sup> There were also complaints against company president Jay Welker, who is also the head of the Wells Fargo wealth management division, due to his sexist statements regarding female employees. The female workers claimed that he called them "girls" and said that they "should be at home taking care of their children."<sup id="cite_ref-Rooney_129-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rooney-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Auto_insurance">Auto insurance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Auto insurance" data-section="40" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On June 10, 2019, Wells Fargo settled a lawsuit for <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">$</a> 385 million that was filed in 2017 concerning their customers and <a href="/wiki/National_General_Insurance" title="National General Insurance">National General Insurance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(4)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="CEO-to-worker_pay_ratio">CEO-to-worker pay ratio</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: CEO-to-worker pay ratio" data-section="41" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-4 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-4">
<p>Pursuant to Section 953(b) of the <a href="/wiki/Dodd-Frank_Wall_Street_Reform_and_Consumer_Protection_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Dodd-Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act">Dodd-Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act</a>, publicly traded companies are required to disclose (1) the median total annual compensation of all employees other than the CEO and (2) the ratio of the CEO’s annual total compensation to that of the median employee.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p><p>Total 2018 compensation for <a href="/wiki/Timothy_J._Sloan" title="Timothy J. Sloan">Timothy J. Sloan</a>, CEO, was $18,426,734, and total compensation for the median employee was estimated to be $65,191. The resulting pay ratio was determined to be 283:1.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(5)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wells_Fargo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: See also" data-section="42" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-5 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-5">

We regret the error.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Correction+Wells+Fargo+private+prison+divestment&amp;rft.pub=National+Prison+Divestment+Campaign&amp;rft.date=2017-11-02&amp;rft.au=Cervantes-Gautschi%2C+Peter&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fprisondivestment.wordpress.com%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWells+Fargo" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r935243608"></span>
